<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Mobile phone</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Portable device to make telephone calls using a radio link</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cell Phone" and "Handphone" redirect here. For the films, see <a href="/wiki/Cell_Phone_(film)" title="Cell Phone (film)">Cell Phone (film)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Handphone_(film)" title="Handphone (film)">Handphone (film)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the modern mobile phone, see <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">Smartphone</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4323" data-file-width="3977" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg/290px-Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg/435px-Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg/580px-Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mobile_Phone_Evolution_1992_-_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two decades of evolution of mobile phones, from a 1992 <a href="/wiki/Motorola_MicroTAC#International_GSM" title="Motorola MicroTAC">Motorola 8900X-2</a> to the 2014 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPhone_6_Plus" title="IPhone 6 Plus">iPhone 6 Plus</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>mobile phone</b>, <b>cellular phone, cell phone</b>, <b>cellphone</b>, <b>handphone</b>, <b>hand phone</b> or <b>pocket phone</b>, sometimes shortened to simply <b>mobile</b>, <b>cell</b>, or just <b>phone</b>, is a portable <a href="/wiki/Telephone" title="Telephone">telephone</a> that can make and receive <a href="/wiki/Telephone_call" title="Telephone call">calls</a> over a <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">radio frequency</a> link while the user is moving within a telephone service area. The radio frequency link establishes a connection to the switching systems of a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_operator" title="Mobile phone operator">mobile phone operator</a>, which provides access to the <a href="/wiki/Public_switched_telephone_network" title="Public switched telephone network">public switched telephone network</a> (PSTN). Modern mobile telephone services use a <a href="/wiki/Cellular_network" title="Cellular network">cellular network</a> architecture and, therefore, mobile telephones are called <i>cellular telephones</i> or <i>cell phones</i> in North America. In addition to <a href="/wiki/Telephony" title="Telephony">telephony</a>, digital mobile phones (<a href="/wiki/2G" title="2G">2G</a>) support a variety of other <a href="/wiki/GSM_services" title="GSM services">services</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Text_messaging" title="Text messaging">text messaging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multimedia_Messaging_Service" title="Multimedia Messaging Service">MMS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">email</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internet_access" title="Internet access">Internet access</a>, short-range wireless communications (<a href="/wiki/Infrared_Data_Association" title="Infrared Data Association">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a>), business applications, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_games" title="Video games">video games</a> and <a href="/wiki/Digital_photography" title="Digital photography">digital photography</a>. Mobile phones offering only those capabilities are known as <a href="/wiki/Feature_phone" title="Feature phone">feature phones</a>; mobile phones which offer greatly advanced computing capabilities are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srivastava_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Srivastava-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal-oxide-semiconductor" title="Metal-oxide-semiconductor">metal-oxide-semiconductor</a> (MOS) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_integration" title="Large-scale integration">large-scale integration</a> (LSI) technology, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_network" title="Cellular network">cellular networking</a> led to the development of affordable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_communications" title="Mobile communications">mobile communications</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srivastava_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Srivastava-1">[1]</a></sup> The first handheld mobile phone was demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Cooper_(inventor)" title="Martin Cooper (inventor)">Martin Cooper</a> of <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in 1973, using a handset weighing c. 2 kilograms (4.4 lbs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inventor_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inventor-2">[2]</a></sup> In 1979, <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Telegraph_and_Telephone" title="Nippon Telegraph and Telephone">Nippon Telegraph and Telephone</a> (NTT) launched the world's first cellular network in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In 1983, the <a href="/wiki/Motorola_DynaTAC" title="Motorola DynaTAC">DynaTAC 8000x</a> was the first commercially available handheld mobile phone. From 1983 to 2014, worldwide mobile phone subscriptions grew to over seven billion; enough to provide one for every person on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITU_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITU-4">[4]</a></sup> In the first quarter of 2016, the top <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a> developers worldwide were <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Electronics" title="Samsung Electronics">Samsung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huawei" title="Huawei">Huawei</a>; smartphone sales represented 78 percent of total mobile phone sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Feature_phone" title="Feature phone">feature phones</a> (<a href="/wiki/Slang" title="Slang">slang</a>: <i>"dumbphones"</i>) as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mobile_phone&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the top-selling brands were Samsung, <a href="/wiki/HMD_Global" title="HMD Global">Nokia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcatel_Mobile" title="Alcatel Mobile">Alcatel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mobile phones are considered an important human invention as it has been one of the most widely used and sold pieces of consumer technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The growth in popularity has been rapid in some places, for example in the UK the total number of mobile phones overtook the number of houses in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Today mobile phones are globally ubiquitous<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and in almost half the world's countries, over 90% of the population own at least one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_mobile_phones" title="History of mobile phones">History of mobile phones</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg/170px-2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg/255px-2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg/340px-2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007Computex_e21Forum-MartinCooper.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_Cooper_(inventor)" title="Martin Cooper (inventor)">Martin Cooper</a> of Motorola, shown here in a 2007 reenactment, made the first publicized handheld mobile phone call on a prototype DynaTAC model on 3 April 1973.</div></div></div>
<p>A handheld mobile radio telephone service was envisioned in the early stages of radio engineering. In 1917, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finnish</a> inventor <a href="/wiki/Eric_Tigerstedt" title="Eric Tigerstedt">Eric Tigerstedt</a> filed a patent for a "pocket-size folding telephone with a very thin carbon microphone". Early predecessors of cellular phones included <a href="/wiki/Analog_signal" title="Analog signal">analog</a> radio communications from ships and trains. The race to create truly portable telephone devices began after World War II, with developments taking place in many countries. The advances in <a href="/wiki/Mobile_telephony" title="Mobile telephony">mobile telephony</a> have been traced in successive "generations", starting with the early zeroth-generation (<a href="/wiki/Mobile_radio_telephone" title="Mobile radio telephone">0G</a>) services, such as <a href="/wiki/Bell_System" title="Bell System">Bell System</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Telephone_Service" title="Mobile Telephone Service">Mobile Telephone Service</a> and its successor, the <a href="/wiki/Improved_Mobile_Telephone_Service" title="Improved Mobile Telephone Service">Improved Mobile Telephone Service</a>. These 0G systems were not <a href="/wiki/Cellular_network" title="Cellular network">cellular</a>, supported few simultaneous calls, and were very expensive.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DynaTAC8000X.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/DynaTAC8000X.jpg/110px-DynaTAC8000X.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/DynaTAC8000X.jpg/165px-DynaTAC8000X.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/DynaTAC8000X.jpg/220px-DynaTAC8000X.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DynaTAC8000X.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Motorola_DynaTAC" title="Motorola DynaTAC">Motorola DynaTAC</a> 8000X. In 1983, it became the first commercially available handheld cellular mobile phone.</div></div></div>
<p>The development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal-oxide-semiconductor" title="Metal-oxide-semiconductor">metal-oxide-semiconductor</a> (MOS) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_integration" title="Large-scale integration">large-scale integration</a> (LSI) technology, <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_network" title="Cellular network">cellular networking</a> led to the development of affordable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_communications" title="Mobile communications">mobile communications</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srivastava_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Srivastava-1">[1]</a></sup> and devices such as the <a href="/wiki/Car_phone" title="Car phone">car phone</a>.  The first handheld cellular mobile phone was demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/John_Francis_Mitchell" title="John Francis Mitchell">John F. Mitchell</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_F._Mitchell_Biography_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_F._Mitchell_Biography-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Who_invented_the_cell_phone_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Who_invented_the_cell_phone-12">[12]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Cooper_(inventor)" title="Martin Cooper (inventor)">Martin Cooper</a> of <a href="/wiki/Motorola_Mobility" title="Motorola Mobility">Motorola</a> in 1973, using a handset weighing 2 kilograms (4.4 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inventor_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Inventor-2">[2]</a></sup> The first commercial automated cellular network (<a href="/wiki/1G" title="1G">1G</a>) <a href="/wiki/Analog_signal" title="Analog signal">analog</a> was launched in Japan by <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Telegraph_and_Telephone" title="Nippon Telegraph and Telephone">Nippon Telegraph and Telephone</a> in 1979. This was followed in 1981 by the simultaneous launch of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Mobile_Telephone" title="Nordic Mobile Telephone">Nordic Mobile Telephone</a> (NMT) system in Denmark, Finland, Norway, and Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>  Several other countries then followed in the early to mid-1980s. These first-generation (<a href="/wiki/1G" title="1G">1G</a>) systems could support far more simultaneous calls but still used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analog_electronics" title="Analog electronics">analog</a> cellular technology. In 1983, the <a href="/wiki/Motorola_DynaTAC" title="Motorola DynaTAC">DynaTAC 8000x</a> was the first commercially available handheld mobile phone.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Digital_electronics" title="Digital electronics">Digital</a> cellular networks appeared in the 1990s, enabled by the wide adoption of <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">MOSFET</a>-based <a href="/wiki/RF_power_amplifier" title="RF power amplifier">RF power amplifiers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Power_MOSFET" title="Power MOSFET">power MOSFET</a> and <a href="/wiki/LDMOS" title="LDMOS">LDMOS</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RF_circuit" title="RF circuit">RF circuits</a> (<a href="/wiki/RF_CMOS" title="RF CMOS">RF CMOS</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baliga_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baliga-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asif_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asif-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neill-16">[16]</a></sup> leading to the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal_processing" title="Digital signal processing">digital signal processing</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_communications" title="Wireless communications">wireless communications</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srivastava_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Srivastava-1">[1]</a></sup> In 1991, the second-generation (<a href="/wiki/2G" title="2G">2G</a>) digital cellular technology was launched in Finland by <a href="/wiki/Radiolinja" title="Radiolinja">Radiolinja</a> on the <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a> standard. This sparked competition in the sector as the new operators challenged the incumbent 1G network operators. The GSM standard is a European initiative expressed at the <a href="/wiki/European_Conference_of_Postal_and_Telecommunications_Administrations" title="European Conference of Postal and Telecommunications Administrations">CEPT</a> ("Conférence Européenne des Postes et Telecommunications", European Postal and Telecommunications conference). The Franco-German R&amp;D cooperation demonstrated the technical feasibility, and in 1987 a Memorandum of Understanding was signed between 13 European countries who agreed to launch a commercial service by 1991. The first version of the GSM (=2G) standard had 6,000 pages. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">IEEE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Society of Edinburgh">RSE</a> awarded to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Haug" title="Thomas Haug">Thomas Haug</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Dupuis_(engineer)" title="Philippe Dupuis (engineer)">Philippe Dupuis</a> the 2018 <a href="/wiki/IEEE/RSE_James_Clerk_Maxwell_Medal" title="IEEE/RSE James Clerk Maxwell Medal">James Clerk Maxwell medal</a> for their contributions to the first digital mobile telephone standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In 2018, the GSM was used by over 5 billion people in over 220 countries. The GSM (2G) has evolved into 3G, 4G and 5G.  The standardisation body for GSM started at the CEPT Working Group GSM (Group Special Mobile) in 1982 under the umbrella of CEPT. In 1988, <a href="/wiki/ETSI" title="ETSI">ETSI</a> was established and all CEPT standardization activities were transferred to ETSI. Working Group GSM became Technical Committee GSM. In 1991, it became Technical Committee SMG (Special Mobile Group) when ETSI tasked the committee with UMTS (3G).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1484" data-file-width="2220" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg/220px-Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg/330px-Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg/440px-Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dupuis_Haug_GSM_around_1990.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dupuis and Haug during a GSM meeting in Belgium, April 1992</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="696" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg/220px-Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg/330px-Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg/440px-Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mobile_phone_PHS_Japan_1997-2003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Personal_Handy-phone_System" title="Personal Handy-phone System">Personal Handy-phone System</a> mobiles and modems, 1997–2003</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion battery</a>, an indispensable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_source" title="Energy source">energy source</a> for modern mobile phones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-18">[18]</a></sup> was commercialized by <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Kasei" title="Asahi Kasei">Asahi Kasei</a> in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sony91_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-sony91-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieee_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieee-20">[20]</a></sup> In 2001, the third generation (<a href="/wiki/3G" title="3G">3G</a>) was launched in Japan by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NTT_DoCoMo" title="NTT DoCoMo">NTT DoCoMo</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WCDMA" title="WCDMA">WCDMA</a> standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This was followed by 3.5G, 3G+ or turbo 3G enhancements based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-Speed_Packet_Access" title="High-Speed Packet Access">high-speed packet access</a> (HSPA) family, allowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Mobile_Telecommunications_System" title="Universal Mobile Telecommunications System">UMTS networks</a> to have higher data transfer speeds and capacity.
</p><p>By 2009, it had become clear that, at some point, 3G networks would be overwhelmed by the growth of bandwidth-intensive applications, such as <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">streaming media</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Consequently, the industry began looking to data-optimized fourth-generation technologies, with the promise of speed improvements up to ten-fold over existing 3G technologies. The first two commercially available technologies billed as <a href="/wiki/4G" title="4G">4G</a> were the <a href="/wiki/WiMAX" title="WiMAX">WiMAX</a> standard, offered in North America by <a href="/wiki/Sprint_Corporation" title="Sprint Corporation">Sprint</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/LTE_(telecommunication)" title="LTE (telecommunication)">LTE</a> standard, first offered in Scandinavia by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TeliaSonera" title="TeliaSonera">TeliaSonera</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/5G" title="5G">5G</a> is a technology and term used in research papers and projects to denote the next major phase in mobile telecommunication standards beyond the <a href="/wiki/4G" title="4G">4G</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IMT-Advanced" title="IMT-Advanced">IMT-Advanced</a> standards. The term 5G is not officially used in any specification or official document yet made public by telecommunication companies or standardization bodies such as <a href="/wiki/3GPP" title="3GPP">3GPP</a>, <a href="/wiki/WiMAX" title="WiMAX">WiMAX</a> Forum or <a href="/wiki/ITU-R" title="ITU-R">ITU-R</a>. New standards beyond 4G are currently being developed by standardization bodies, but they are at this time seen as under the 4G umbrella, not for a new mobile generation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg/350px-Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg/525px-Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg/700px-Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Active_mobile_broadband_subscriptions_2007-2014.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Active mobile broadband subscriptions per 100 inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smartphone">Smartphone</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">Smartphone</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">Smartphones</a> have a number of distinguishing features.  The <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">International Telecommunication Union</a> measures those with Internet connection, which it calls <i>Active Mobile-Broadband subscriptions</i> (which includes tablets, etc.). In the developed world, smartphones have now overtaken the usage of earlier mobile systems. However, in the developing world, they account for around 50% of <a href="/wiki/Mobile_telephony" title="Mobile telephony">mobile telephony</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feature_phone">Feature phone</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feature_phone" title="Feature phone">Feature phone</a></div>
<p>Feature phone is a term typically used as a <a href="/wiki/Retronym" title="Retronym">retronym</a> to describe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_phones" title="Mobile phones">mobile phones</a> which are limited in capabilities in contrast to a modern <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a>. Feature phones typically provide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voice_calling" title="Voice calling">voice calling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Text_messaging" title="Text messaging">text messaging</a> functionality, in addition to basic <a href="/wiki/Multimedia" title="Multimedia">multimedia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> capabilities, and other services offered by the user's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_service_provider" title="Wireless service provider">wireless service provider</a>. A feature phone has additional functions over and above a basic mobile phone, which is only capable of voice calling and text messaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes-twoweeks_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-twoweeks-25">[25]</a></sup> Feature phones and basic mobile phones tend to use a proprietary, custom-designed <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> and <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">user interface</a>. By contrast, smartphones generally use a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_operating_system" title="Mobile operating system">mobile operating system</a> that often shares common traits across devices.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cellular_network" title="Cellular network">Cellular network</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WiFi" title="WiFi">WiFi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frequency_reuse.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="279" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Frequency_reuse.svg/400px-Frequency_reuse.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Frequency_reuse.svg/600px-Frequency_reuse.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Frequency_reuse.svg/800px-Frequency_reuse.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frequency_reuse.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cellular networks work by only reusing radio frequencies (in this example frequencies f1-f4) in non adjacent cells to avoid interference</div></div></div>
<p>Mobile phones communicate with cell towers that are placed to give coverage across a telephone service area, which is divided up into 'cells'. Each cell uses a different set of frequencies from neighboring cells, and will typically be covered by three towers placed at different locations. The cell towers are usually interconnected to each other and the phone network and the internet by wired connections. Due to bandwidth limitations each cell will have a maximum number of cell phones it can handle at once. The cells are therefore sized depending on the expected usage density, and may be much smaller in cities. In that case much lower transmitter powers are used to avoid broadcasting beyond the cell.
</p><p>In order to handle the high traffic, multiple towers can be set up in the same area (using different frequencies). This can be done permanently or temporarily such as at special events like at the Super Bowl, Taste of Chicago, State Fair, NYC New Year's Eve, hurricane hit cities, etc. where cell phone companies will bring a truck with equipment to host the abnormally high traffic with a portable cell.
</p><p>Cellular can greatly increase the capacity of simultaneous wireless phone calls. While a phone company for example, has a license to 1,000 frequencies, each cell must use unique frequencies with each call using one of them when communicating. Because cells only slightly overlap, the same frequency can be reused. Example cell one uses frequency 1–500, next door cell uses frequency 501–1,000, next door can reuse frequency 1–500. Cells one and three are not "touching" and do not overlap/communicate so each can reuse the same frequencies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Capacity was further increased when phone companies implemented digital networks. With digital, one frequency can host multiple simultaneous calls.
</p><p>As a phone moves around, a phone will "hand off" - automatically disconnect and reconnect to the tower of another cell that gives the best reception.
</p><p>Additionally, short-range <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> infrastructure is often used by smartphones as much as possible as it offloads traffic from cell networks on to local area networks.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware">Hardware</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_features" title="Mobile phone features">Mobile phone features</a></div>
<p>The common components found on all mobile phones are:
</p>
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processing unit</a> (CPU), the processor of phones. The CPU is a <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessor</a> fabricated on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal%E2%80%93oxide%E2%80%93semiconductor" title="Metal–oxide–semiconductor">metal–oxide–semiconductor</a> (MOS) <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuit</a> (IC) chip.</li>
<li>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a>, providing the power source for the phone functions. A modern handset typically uses a <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion battery</a> (LIB), whereas older handsets used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93metal_hydride" title="Nickel–metal hydride">nickel–metal hydride</a> (Ni–MH) batteries.</li>
<li>An input mechanism to allow the user to interact with the phone. These are a <a href="/wiki/Keypad" title="Keypad">keypad</a> for feature phones, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touch_screens" title="Touch screens">touch screens</a> for most smartphones (typically with <a href="/wiki/Capacitive_sensing" title="Capacitive sensing">capacitive sensing</a>).</li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Display_device" title="Display device">display</a> which echoes the user's typing, and displays text messages, contacts, and more. The display is typically either a <a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">liquid-crystal display</a> (LCD) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_light-emitting_diode" title="Organic light-emitting diode">organic light-emitting diode</a> (OLED) display.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Loudspeaker" title="Loudspeaker">Speakers</a> for sound.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subscriber_identity_module" title="Subscriber identity module">Subscriber identity module</a> (SIM) cards and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Removable_user_identity_module&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Removable user identity module (page does not exist)">removable user identity module</a> (R-UIM) cards.</li>
<li>A hardware <a href="/wiki/Notification_LED" title="Notification LED">notification LED</a> on some phones</li></ul>
<p>Low-end mobile phones are often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Feature_phone" title="Feature phone">feature phones</a> and offer basic telephony. Handsets with more advanced computing ability through the use of native software applications are known as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_processing_unit">Central processing unit</span></h3>
<p>Mobile phones have <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processing units</a> (CPUs), similar to those in computers, but optimised to operate in low power environments.
</p><p>Mobile CPU performance depends not only on the clock rate (generally given in multiples of <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> but also the <a href="/wiki/Memory_hierarchy" title="Memory hierarchy">memory hierarchy</a> also greatly affects overall performance. Because of these problems, the performance of mobile phone CPUs is often more appropriately given by scores derived from various standardized tests to measure the real effective performance in commonly used applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Display">Display</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Display_device" title="Display device">Display device</a></div>
<p>One of the main characteristics of phones is the <a href="/wiki/Display_device" title="Display device">screen</a>. Depending on the device's type and design, the screen fills most or nearly all of the space on a device's front surface. Many smartphone displays have an <a href="/wiki/Display_aspect_ratio" title="Display aspect ratio">aspect ratio</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/16:9" title="16:9">16:9</a>, but taller aspect ratios became more common in 2017.
</p><p>Screen sizes are often measured in diagonal <a href="/wiki/Inch" title="Inch">inches</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millimeter" title="Millimeter">millimeters</a>; feature phones generally have screen sizes below 90 millimetres (3.5 in). Phones with screens larger than 130 millimetres (5.2 in) are often called "<a href="/wiki/Phablet" title="Phablet">phablets</a>." Smartphones with screens over 115 millimetres (4.5 in) in size are commonly difficult to use with only a single hand, since most thumbs cannot reach the entire screen surface; they may need to be shifted around in the hand, held in one hand and manipulated by the other, or used in place with both hands. Due to design advances, some modern smartphones with large screen sizes and "edge-to-edge" designs have compact builds that improve their ergonomics, while the shift to taller aspect ratios have resulted in phones that have larger screen sizes whilst maintaining the ergonomics associated with smaller 16:9 displays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phonearena-s7edgenotphablet_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-phonearena-s7edgenotphablet-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-phabletpytha_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-phabletpytha-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-s8aspectratio_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-s8aspectratio-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">Liquid-crystal displays</a> are the most common; others are <a href="/wiki/IPS_panel" title="IPS panel">IPS</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LED</a>, <a href="/wiki/OLED" title="OLED">OLED</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AMOLED" title="AMOLED">AMOLED</a> displays. Some displays are integrated with pressure-sensitive digitizers, such as those developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wacom_(company)" title="Wacom (company)">Wacom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and Apple's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3D_Touch" title="3D Touch">3D Touch</a>" system.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound">Sound</span></h3>
<p>In sound, smartphones and feature phones vary little. Some audio-quality enhancing features, such as <a href="/wiki/Voice_over_LTE" title="Voice over LTE">Voice over LTE</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HD_Voice" title="HD Voice">HD Voice</a>, have appeared and are often available on newer smartphones.  Sound quality can remain a problem due to the design of the phone, the quality of the cellular network and compression algorithms used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-distance_call" title="Long-distance call">long-distance calls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>  Audio quality can be improved using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VoIP" title="VoIP">VoIP</a> application over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WiFi" title="WiFi">WiFi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Cellphones have small speakers so that the user can use a <a href="/wiki/Speakerphone" title="Speakerphone">speakerphone</a> feature and talk to a person on the phone without holding it to their ear. The small speakers can also be used to listen to digital audio files of music or speech or watch videos with an audio component, without holding the phone close to the ear.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battery">Battery</span></h3>
<p>The average phone battery lasts 2–3 years at best. Many of the wireless devices use a Lithium-Ion (Li-Ion) battery, which charges 500–2500 times, depending on how users take care of the battery and the charging techniques used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> It is only natural for these rechargeable batteries to chemically age, which is why the performance of the battery when used for a year or two will begin to deteriorate. Battery life can be extended by draining it regularly, not overcharging it, and keeping it away from heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="SIM_card">SIM card</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subscriber_Identity_Module" title="Subscriber Identity Module">Subscriber Identity Module</a> and <a href="/wiki/Removable_User_Identity_Module" title="Removable User Identity Module">Removable User Identity Module</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1114" data-file-width="862" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg/170px-Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg/255px-Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg/340px-Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Typical_cellphone_SIM_cards.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical mobile phone mini-<a href="/wiki/SIM_card" title="SIM card">SIM card</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mobile phones require a small <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">microchip</a> called a Subscriber Identity Module or <a href="/wiki/SIM_card" title="SIM card">SIM card</a>, in order to function. The SIM card is approximately the size of a small postage stamp and is usually placed underneath the battery in the rear of the unit. The SIM securely stores the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Mobile_Subscriber_Identity" title="International Mobile Subscriber Identity">service-subscriber key (IMSI)</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authentication_Center" title="Authentication Center">K<sub>i</sub></a> used to identify and authenticate the user of the mobile phone. The SIM card allows users to change phones by simply removing the SIM card from one mobile phone and inserting it into another mobile phone or broadband telephony device, provided that this is not prevented by a <a href="/wiki/SIM_lock" title="SIM lock">SIM lock</a>. The first SIM card was made in 1991 by Munich smart card maker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giesecke_%26_Devrient" title="Giesecke &amp; Devrient">Giesecke &amp; Devrient</a> for the Finnish wireless network operator <a href="/wiki/Radiolinja" title="Radiolinja">Radiolinja</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A hybrid mobile phone can hold up to four SIM cards, with a phone having a different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IMEI" title="IMEI">device identifier</a> for each SIM Card. SIM and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R-UIM" title="R-UIM">R-UIM</a> cards may be mixed together to allow both <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CDMA" title="CDMA">CDMA</a> networks to be accessed. From 2010 onwards, such phones became popular in emerging markets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and this was attributed to the desire to obtain the lowest calling costs.
</p><p>When the removal of a SIM card is detected by the operating system, it may deny further operation until a reboot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Software">Software</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_platforms">Software platforms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Android_phones.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Android_phones.jpg/220px-Android_phones.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Android_phones.jpg/330px-Android_phones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Android_phones.jpg/440px-Android_phones.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Android_phones.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Android smartphones</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mobile_operating_system" title="Mobile operating system">Mobile operating system</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p>Feature phones have basic software platforms. Smartphones have advanced software platforms. <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android OS</a> has been the <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_operating_systems" title="Usage share of operating systems">best-selling OS</a> worldwide on smartphones since 2011.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_app">Mobile app</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">Mobile app</a></div>
<p>A mobile app is a computer program designed to run on a mobile device, such as a smartphone. The term "app" is a shortening of the term "software application".
</p>
<dl><dt>Messaging</dt></dl>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">SMS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multimedia_Messaging_Service" title="Multimedia Messaging Service">MMS</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg/220px-IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg/330px-IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg/440px-IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A text message (SMS)</div></div></div>
<p>A common data application on mobile phones is <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">Short Message Service</a> (SMS) text messaging. The first SMS message was sent from a computer to a mobile phone in 1992 in the UK while the first person-to-person SMS from phone to phone was sent in Finland in 1993. The first <a href="/wiki/Mobile_news" title="Mobile news">mobile news</a> service, delivered via SMS, was launched in Finland in 2000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and subsequently many organizations provided "on-demand" and "instant" news services by SMS.  <a href="/wiki/Multimedia_Messaging_Service" title="Multimedia Messaging Service">Multimedia Messaging Service</a> (MMS) was introduced in March 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_stores">Application stores</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_mobile_app_distribution_platforms" title="List of mobile app distribution platforms">List of mobile app distribution platforms</a></div>
<p>The introduction of Apple's App Store for the iPhone and iPod Touch in July 2008 popularized manufacturer-hosted <a href="/wiki/List_of_mobile_app_distribution_platforms" title="List of mobile app distribution platforms">online distribution</a> for third-party applications (software and computer programs) focused on a single platform. There are a huge variety of apps, including <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video games</a>, music products and business tools. Up until that point, smartphone application distribution depended on <a href="/wiki/List_of_mobile_app_distribution_platforms#Third-party_platforms" title="List of mobile app distribution platforms">third-party sources</a> providing applications for multiple platforms, such as <a href="/wiki/GetJar" title="GetJar">GetJar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Handango" title="Handango">Handango</a>, <a href="/wiki/Handmark" title="Handmark">Handmark</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PocketGear" title="PocketGear">PocketGear</a>. Following the success of the App Store, other smartphone manufacturers launched application stores, such as Google's Android Market (later renamed to the Google Play Store), RIM's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BlackBerry_App_World" title="BlackBerry App World">BlackBerry App World</a>, or Android-related app stores like <a href="/wiki/Aptoide" title="Aptoide">Aptoide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cafe_Bazaar" title="Cafe Bazaar">Cafe Bazaar</a>, <a href="/wiki/F-Droid" title="F-Droid">F-Droid</a>, <a href="/wiki/GetJar" title="GetJar">GetJar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Opera_Mobile_Store" title="Opera Mobile Store">Opera Mobile Store</a>. In February 2014, 93% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_developers" title="Mobile developers">mobile developers</a> were targeting smartphones first for mobile app development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales">Sales</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_manufacturer">By manufacturer</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_mobile_phones" title="List of best-selling mobile phones">List of best-selling mobile phones</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_mobile_phone_brands_by_country" title="List of mobile phone brands by country">List of mobile phone brands by country</a></div>
